    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";

          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer6472989732581199979">A short story by Fyodor Dostoyevsky written in 1877. It begins with a man walking St. Petersburg's streets while musing upon how ridiculous his life is, as well as its distinct lack of meaning or purpose. This train of thought leads him to the idea of suicide, which he resolves to commit using a previously-acquired gun. However, a chance encounter with a distressed little </span>
  <span id="freeText6472989732581199979" style="display:none">A short story by Fyodor Dostoyevsky written in 1877. It begins with a man walking St. Petersburg's streets while musing upon how ridiculous his life is, as well as its distinct lack of meaning or purpose. This train of thought leads him to the idea of suicide, which he resolves to commit using a previously-acquired gun. However, a chance encounter with a distressed little girl in the street derails his drastic plans.</span>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_775857999" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/775857999" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/775857999?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer775857999" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9812380215728450366"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1549414309i/27020832._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The intensity of the long novels of Dostoevsky is compressed in the novella <i>Notes from Underground</i> where the underground man narrates his tale of spite and nastiness in one hundred and thirty pages. However, the intensity is compressed even more, in a kind of super-intensity, as the ridiculous man narrates his fantastic, hallucinogenic tale in the twenty page story <i>The Dream of a Ridiculous Man</i>. <br/><br/>I first read this story years ago and the images made a deep impression. I just did complete rereadin</span>
  <span id="freeText9812380215728450366" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1549414309i/27020832._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The intensity of the long novels of Dostoevsky is compressed in the novella <i>Notes from Underground</i> where the underground man narrates his tale of spite and nastiness in one hundred and thirty pages. However, the intensity is compressed even more, in a kind of super-intensity, as the ridiculous man narrates his fantastic, hallucinogenic tale in the twenty page story <i>The Dream of a Ridiculous Man</i>. <br><br>I first read this story years ago and the images made a deep impression. I just did complete rereading the story five times over and each reading made a progressively deeper impression. I recall Isaac Stern saying how one string quartet of Franz Schubert was absolutely perfect, that is, the piece was composed so exactly every single note fit with every other note. That is the way I feel about this work of Dostoevsky - even in English translation, every single sentence, every word even, is a jewel and is perfectly set to work within the story as a whole. What a masterpiece!<br><br>We read right at the beginning how the ridiculous man knows he himself is ridiculous and reflects on life as follows: "Perhaps it was owing to the terrible misery that was growing in my soul through something which was of more consequence than anything else about me: that something was the conviction that had come upon me that nothing in the world mattered." <br><br>We follow the ridiculous man as he walks the dank streets during a gloomy, rainy evening, and, seeing a star in a particular way, knows this is the evening he will kill himself. Unexpectedly, he then has an encounter with a terrorized, soaked eight year old girl. We follow the ridiculous man back to his flat where he sits in solitude in his chair with his revolver on the table. The ridiculous man thinks his ridiculous thoughts, "It seemed clear to me that life and the world somehow depended on me. I may almost say that the world now seemed created for me along; if I shot myself the world would cease to be at least for me." Who hasn't had their own stream of thought follow a similar logic, even if we sit in our chair without a revolver on the table? Dostoevsky knows the soul of man so well!<br><br>The ridiculous man falls asleep in his chair and has his unforgettable dream, which, in a way, is a crystallization and elaboration of the fragmentary dreams every one of us have experienced, either consciously or unconsciously. "And now I was buried in the earth. They all went away, I was left along, utterly alone." After being lead to a world very much like our own, the ridiculous man encounters a people and tells us, "And at last I saw and knew the people of this happy land. They came to me of themselves, they surrounded me, kissed me." Experiencing the beauty of these people, the ridiculous man tells us, "They liked making songs about one another, and praised each other like children; they were the simplest songs, but they sprang from their hearts and went to one's heart." <br><br>On a personal note, this is one image I remember from my first reading years ago. Perhaps the reason is my own abiding memory of growing up was how everybody mocked and discounted and belittled one another. As a youth, taking the words and attitudes of those around me, I did the same thing. It was a kind of cultural infection, a sickness, and it wasn't until I was in college that I saw it for exactly what it was - a sickness. As usual, Dostoevsky has his finger right on the pulse here.<br><br>I wouldn't want to spoil the ending for anybody by telling how the dream continues and finally ends, but I would like to quote what the ridiculous man says to us toward the end, "And yet it's an old truth which has been told and retold a billion times - but it has not formed part of our lives!" What is the old truth? What has not formed part of our lives? To find out exactly, please read and plumb the depths of your own soul as you follow the ridiculous man on his miraculous journey.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1549414309i/27020833._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="9812380215728450366" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_752849525" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/752849525" />
    <a title="Florencia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia"><img alt="Florencia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601866797p2/21575078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/752849525?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Florencia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Florencia" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia">Florencia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=russian">russian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=dostoyevskyesqueasdfdsfd-ism">dostoyevskyesqueasdfdsfd-ism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=stories-and-novellas-for-this-life">stories-and-novellas-for-this-life</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer752849525" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8465444419758064646"><blockquote><i>‘You’re not Dostoevsky,’ said the citizeness, who was getting muddled by Koroviev.<br/>‘Well, who knows, who knows,’ he replied.<br/>‘Dostoevsky’s dead,’ said the citizeness, but somehow not very confidently.<br/>‘I protest!’ Behemoth exclaimed hotly. ‘Dostoevsky is immortal!’</i> <br/>― Mikhail Bulgakov, <i>The Master and Margarita</i></blockquote><br/>I said it once and I'll say it again. This man knew and understood human nature like no one. His ability to explore the human psyche was outstanding. He wrote about every subject concerning hum</span>
  <span id="freeText8465444419758064646" style="display:none"><blockquote><i>‘You’re not Dostoevsky,’ said the citizeness, who was getting muddled by Koroviev.<br />‘Well, who knows, who knows,’ he replied.<br />‘Dostoevsky’s dead,’ said the citizeness, but somehow not very confidently.<br />‘I protest!’ Behemoth exclaimed hotly. ‘Dostoevsky is immortal!’</i> <br />― Mikhail Bulgakov, <i>The Master and Margarita</i></blockquote><br />I said it once and I'll say it again. This man knew and understood human nature like no one. His ability to explore the human psyche was outstanding. He wrote about every subject concerning human nature and he did it beautifully. This is the kind of writer who speaks to my soul, who helps me open my eyes and see things from a different perspective, who leaves me with this giant void ironically full of doubts in this absurd little world of ours and makes me think: “We should totally be friends.”<br />I love this writer. And I enjoyed this book; most of it, at least.<br /><br />This short story is about a pessimist, apathetic man (he described himself as “ridiculous”) who has been thinking about shooting himself for two months. Then, one rainy night, he made up his mind and decided to do it. While walking home, a girl came across and asked for his help; apparently, her mother wasn't feeling well. He rejected her and even insulted her, and went straight home. At first, that cruel passage kind of shocked me. We are talking about the same kind of indifference towards the world that Meursault felt (in my opinion), but somehow I found Dostoyevsky's protagonist dreadfully indifferent, even though the other one <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[didn't show any emotion after his mother died and killed an Arab because it was a sunny day <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> (but, still, other circumstances). He later explained why he acted that way. And then, he just couldn't stop thinking about that little girl. That fact delayed his suicide. Until he fell asleep and had a quite realistic dream. In that dream, he shot himself and realized he was dead; he was in a coffin, surrounded by darkness, unable to move. However, he could think, he was aware of everything that was going on around him. A miserable situation. And quite irritating, too. The man killed himself for <i>a reason</i>. No more existence, no more feeling, no more thinking. Imagine committing suicide only to find out that you'll keep thinking inside a coffin, for eternity. That would be hell, indeed. Suddenly, our world looks like paradise.<br /><br />Anyway, more stuff happened. Conclusion? That dream changed the man. He started to look at life from a different point of view. It is a simple idea but well-executed. However, personally, I felt awkward while reading this. And I know, I complain about everything. I want a better world but when I read about it, I feel awkward, almost annoyed. Still, there is just so much LOVE in this short story that... I don't know. I loved the first part of it, but then the narrator's spiritual awakening takes over the whole thing and everything is so sweet and cheery, from perfect people shedding tears of joy to Love-a-Lot Bears vomiting rainbows made of honey. "They liked to compose songs about each other and praised each other like children." … You have to read it to understand...<br />That was a torturous chapter for me. Then, it got better. Then, preachy.<br /><br />Anyhow, this is still a Dostoyevsky's story. And there are many insightful reflections to enjoy, as usual. Some beautifully written sentences that describe human nature in all its glory. <br /><blockquote>When they became wicked, they began to talk of brotherhood and humaneness and understood these ideas. When they became criminal, they invented justice and prescribed whole codices for themselves in order to maintain it, and to ensure the codices they set up the guillotine. (12)</blockquote><br />Dostoyevsky, you are immortal.<br /><br /><br />May 25, 14 (apparently)<br />* Also on <a href="https://nonbookreviews.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">my blog</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8465444419758064646" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elyse  Walters" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters">Elyse  Walters</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2753065578" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1597751614118214741">I don’t regularly pick books to read by the famous legendary Russian novelist...<br/>Fyodor Mikhailovich Dostoyevsky ....<br/>but sometimes a girl just needs to do what she feels compelled to do.<br/><br/>I was first enchanted by the title and book cover. <br/>I wanted to know more...<br/>“The Dream of A Ridiculous Man, took about 40 minutes to read. ( a far lot shorter than Crime and Punishment). <br/><br/>Dostoyévsky’s - ( 1821- 1835), works of fiction include 15 novels, and novellas, 17 short stories and 5 transactions. <br/><br/>“The Dream </span>
  <span id="freeText1597751614118214741" style="display:none">I don’t regularly pick books to read by the famous legendary Russian novelist...<br />Fyodor Mikhailovich Dostoyevsky ....<br />but sometimes a girl just needs to do what she feels compelled to do.<br /><br />I was first enchanted by the title and book cover. <br />I wanted to know more...<br />“The Dream of A Ridiculous Man, took about 40 minutes to read. ( a far lot shorter than Crime and Punishment). <br /><br />Dostoyévsky’s - ( 1821- 1835), works of fiction include 15 novels, and novellas, 17 short stories and 5 transactions. <br /><br />“The Dream of A Ridiculous<br />Man” is a powerful deeply felt story.<br /><br />The short story of this short story is about man who has been thinking about killing himself for two months. <br />Then one gloomy night he sees a bright star in the sky and decides ‘this is the night’. His plan was to go home and put a revolver to his head....<br />but as all good plans go - our good intentions - he was thwarted — <br />by a child....and a dream...<br /><br />From dark to light ... an awakening of such - <br />this wise little gem is a tribute to humanity - to life with all its suffering and love ...<br /><br />Without giving away the journey- or the ending - before I drift off to sleep in a few minutes ...<br />I’ll just say....<br />...that this story could provide plenty to discuss - in a book group for the full couple of hours easily ... <br />as much as a book three times as long. <br /><br />I’m reminded - by past memories - that nobody could snap me out of self-critical worthless feelings faster than a child’s suffering. <br /><br />“On our earth we can only love with suffering and through suffering. We cannot love otherwise, and we know of no other sort of love. I want suffering in order to love. I along, I thirst, this very instant, to kiss with tears the earth that I have left, and I don’t want, I won’t except life on any other!”<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1597751614118214741" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer9471764927861777750"><b>“How hard it is to be the only one who understands…the more I learned the more thoroughly I understood that I was ridiculous”</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1573243876i/28423104.jpg' alt='Image result for dostoevsky ridiculous man animation painted' width='480' height='360' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The confessional style of this short story reminded me of Notes from Underground. The protagonists from both stories are, of course, tortured. However, the Underground Man cares deeply what other people think of him. That’s one of the ways in which he suffers. The Ridiculous Man is indifferent to other people and to life itself until a little girl pleads for his help. Then</span>
  <span id="freeText9471764927861777750" style="display:none"><b>“How hard it is to be the only one who understands…the more I learned the more thoroughly I understood that I was ridiculous”</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1573243876i/28423104.jpg" alt="Image result for dostoevsky ridiculous man animation painted" width="480" height="360" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The confessional style of this short story reminded me of Notes from Underground. The protagonists from both stories are, of course, tortured. However, the Underground Man cares deeply what other people think of him. That’s one of the ways in which he suffers. The Ridiculous Man is indifferent to other people and to life itself until a little girl pleads for his help. Then, instead of killing himself as he had planned, he has a dream. In the dream, he does kill himself and is transported to a place where no sin has been committed. His influence on the people here is quite interesting!! 3.75 stars</span>

<div id="review_1721687141" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1721687141" />
    <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan"><img alt="Sidharth Vardhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595750367p2/58296635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1721687141?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sidharth Vardhan" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan">Sidharth Vardhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=sad-lonely-thinking-of-suicide">sad-lonely-thinking-of-suicide</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=7-russian">7-russian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=bestest">bestest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=shorts-readable-online">shorts-readable-online</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1721687141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15330366309694259593"><blockquote>
  <br/>
  <i>“Only perhaps in our children, in their earliest years, one might find, some remote faint reflection of this beauty."</i>
  <br/>
</blockquote><br/>Do you remember losing that treasured innocence that we were born with? that old childish ‘innocence’ (there might be a better word to describe it, but my vocabulary is poor) - the nausea of which we live with for rest of our lives? We know, or at least we think we know, that it can’t be helped, and we would consider someone a weakling, a divine fool or ridiculous if he </span>
  <span id="freeText15330366309694259593" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“Only perhaps in our children, in their earliest years, one might find, some remote faint reflection of this beauty."</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />Do you remember losing that treasured innocence that we were born with? that old childish ‘innocence’ (there might be a better word to describe it, but my vocabulary is poor) - the nausea of which we live with for rest of our lives? We know, or at least we think we know, that it can’t be helped, and we would consider someone a weakling, a divine fool or ridiculous if he or she retained that innocence beyond a certain age. We even lough at our own foolishness of old days:<br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />"They hardly remembered what they had lost, in fact refused to believe that they had ever been happy and innocent. They even laughed at the possibility of this happiness in the past, and called it a dream."</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />Yet, we look at children, ever cheerful, and feel sorry for the loss they are bound to suffer one time or another in their lives – they, too, will eat that fruit from 'tree of knowledge of good and evil' – which will show itself to them in some hardship of life or some light jest or some injustice too brutal for their innocence; and get thrown out from that imaginary paradise which n their ignorance, they had so naturally surround themselves with till then; only to discover inferno of reality - where noble-hearted Abels will be killed and Cains, realizing the injustice inherit in the world will revenge themselves by contributing to the very injustice that had repelled them, and be marked in a way that will make them, or at least make them act as if they were, indifferent to (if not actively causing) sufferings of others. <br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />"Oh, at first perhaps it began innocently, with a jest, coquetry, with amorous play, perhaps indeed with a germ, but that germ of falsity made its way into their hearts and pleased them. Then sensuality was soon begotten, sensuality begot jealousy, jealousy — cruelty … Oh, I don't know, I don't remember; but soon, very soon the first blood was shed."</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />Some of them will fight for their innocence with all their might, but that fight itself shall slowly kill the innocence they are fighting for. The only way, it would seem, to stay innocent is to die young.<br /><br />Our ridiculous man (r-man) is one such person whose failure to fight for innocence has left him with this same indifference we mentioned – an indifference that came so dangerously close to Camus’ Stranger; and turned him into a Nihilist. Tormented minds have always reasoned themselves into finding everything meaningless. <br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“But since I grew to manhood, I have for some unknown reason become calmer, though I realized my awful characteristic more fully every year. I say 'unknown', for to this day I cannot tell why it was. Perhaps it was owing to the terrible misery that was growing in my soul through something which was of more consequence than anything else about me: that something was the conviction that had come upon me that nothing in the world mattered.”</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />Having given up on the world and since nothing whatsoever matters, he is planning to kill himself – when a little girl in misery invokes his sympathy. The discovery that he still had sympathy for her in him, frustrated him and made him treat her badly. Why should he, who already has that mark of indifference, feel sorry for her? His reason told him, nothing <i>should</i> matter – and yet, it <i>did</i> matter. <br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />"You see, though nothing mattered to me, I could feel pain, for instance. If anyone had stuck me it would have hurt me. It was the same morally: if anything very pathetic happened, I should have felt pity just as I used to do in old days when there were things in life that did matter to me. I had felt pity that evening. I should have certainly helped a child. Why, then, had I not helped the little girl? Because of an idea that occurred to me at the time: when she was calling and pulling at me, a question suddenly arose before me and I could not settle it. The question was an idle one, but I was vexed. I was vexed at the reflection that if I were going to make an end of myself that night, nothing in life ought to have mattered to me. Why was it that all at once I did not feel a strange pang, quite incongruous in my position."</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />FD, as we know, has a dislike for rationalism. As a rationalist, r-man wanted to kill himself by shooting his brain – since that is where, he must have thought, his ‘self’ lies – but after the incidence; in his dream, it is his heart he shoots. A person is not what he thinks but rather what he feels.<br /><br />FD knows we can’t argue with our feelings. We can’t reason ourselves into or out of feeling a thing. Rashkilonav’s reasoning had always told him that he never did anything wrong; and yet that reasoning couldn’t save him from feeling guilt. <br /><br />Feelings on the other hand will always devise reasons of their own, even if those reasons may sound ridiculous to others. This time it was done through a dream:<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“Dreams seem to be spurred on not by reason but by desire, not by the head but by the heart, and yet what complicated tricks my reason has played sometimes in dreams, what utterly incomprehensible things happen to it!</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />In a battle between heart and brain, heart wins - each time; at least for FD. FD would argue the innocence, regarding the loss of which we just talked about, is never completely loss; people just cover it with thick black veils of indifference – and forget all about it.<br /><br />Yet, sooner or later – the compassion arising from suffering of a child, the lure of love, sight of something beautiful, colors of festival or warnings from Christmas ghosts (I know last is not FD but I’m sure he won’t mind) will rush it back to surface, no matter how thick that veil of indifference was. <br /><br />Anyway, it was sudden discovery of this innocence, caused in form of compassion for the little girl, that brought with itself a subconscious wish –a wish that manifested itself in his dream. A dream that we all have dreamed – of a Utopia, of a paradise where you never have to grow up. How beautiful itself it was! How easily built! And how unstable! <br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />"As they became wicked they began talking of brotherhood and humanitarianism, and understood those ideas. As they became criminal, they invented justice and drew up whole legal codes in order to observe it, and to ensure their being kept, set up a guillotine." <br /><br />“And yet how simple it is: in one day, in one hour everything could be arranged at once! The chief thing is to love others like yourself, that’s the chief thing, and that’s everything; nothing else is wanted — you will find out at once how to arrange it all.”<br /></i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br /><br />P.S. If you go to Socyd’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1347897390?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">review</a>, you will find link to an awesome animation of the story.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15330366309694259593" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2887328207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1785239318380835578">Fyodor Dostoyevsky is quite a reading experience. After my somewhat dubious encounter with <b>Crime and Punishment</b> a few years back, I wasn't sure I'd undertake another FD novel, but then this short story appeared on my Goodread's feed and I thought....it's only 40 pages!<p> Ha! Throughout the entire story of extremely small print pages, my first thought was, this guy is writing at 100 miles per hour like his thoughts were just exploding from his mind....or that's how it felt to me. His writing so int</p></span>
  <span id="freeText1785239318380835578" style="display:none">Fyodor Dostoyevsky is quite a reading experience. After my somewhat dubious encounter with <b>Crime and Punishment</b> a few years back, I wasn't sure I'd undertake another FD novel, but then this short story appeared on my Goodread's feed and I thought....it's only 40 pages!<p> Ha! Throughout the entire story of extremely small print pages, my first thought was, this guy is writing at 100 miles per hour like his thoughts were just exploding from his mind....or that's how it felt to me. His writing so intense, the story so deep that it was almost as if FD was the Ridiculous Man and the dream had happened to him.</p><p> At first, I didn't like the character or his pathetic view of the world, especially after his treatment of the desperate little girl, but I knew he too was desperate and troubled as we learn early on of his suicidal plan <i>(no spoiler here)</i> so did feel empathy for him.</p><p> Then, after the spiritual dream and his reflection of having seen the truth, his turnabout kind of reminded me of Scrooge and his awakening with a total new outlook on life and the world. Simplistic comparison, but how I will remember this complex character and philosophic read with its religious undertones.</p><p> The message here, for me, is love others as you love yourself and enjoy every day above ground to the fullest.</p><p> Was so glad for the last line in the novella.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="1785239318380835578" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_328530601" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/328530601" />
    <a title="Rajat Ubhaykar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4686064-rajat-ubhaykar"><img alt="Rajat Ubhaykar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352663955p2/4686064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/328530601?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rajat Ubhaykar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rajat Ubhaykar" href="/user/show/4686064-rajat-ubhaykar">Rajat Ubhaykar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer328530601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14627838253840186903">I would rather call this short story a lament on the following grounds:<br/> a) It admits it cannot change things but proceeds to tell us what it thinks anyway. <br/> b) It starts off on a morose note and diligently maintains the tone throughout. <br/><br/>However this is not an ordinary lament. It's an out and out Christian lament that rages against science, rationality and man's relentless quest towards greater understanding. It denounces earth as a place where the "consciousness of life is higher than life, wher</span>
  <span id="freeText14627838253840186903" style="display:none">I would rather call this short story a lament on the following grounds:<br /> a) It admits it cannot change things but proceeds to tell us what it thinks anyway. <br /> b) It starts off on a morose note and diligently maintains the tone throughout. <br /><br />However this is not an ordinary lament. It's an out and out Christian lament that rages against science, rationality and man's relentless quest towards greater understanding. It denounces earth as a place where the "consciousness of life is higher than life, where the knowledge of the laws of happiness is higher than happiness". And other Christian mumbo jumbo. As a man of science, I can't abide by such talk even if it's Dostoyevsky who's doing the talking.<br /><br />This is how the plot unfolds. The unnamed protagonist has been looking at the darker side of things since he was a seven year old kid. Let's call him Gloominov. Gloominov wears apathy as armour and finds little pleasure or meaning in mundane trivialities. One particularly gloomy evening, as he is walking down a particularly gloomy street, the sight of a lone star studded in an inky sky imbues him with a clear purpose. He decides to shoot himself in the head with a shiny new revolver he bought two months ago for the very same purpose. Just then a little girl appears at his feet. She tugs at his pants and grovels and pleads with him to save his dying Mamma. In his deathly haze, he proceeds to discard morality as a useless burden that people who intend to live any further carry inside their equally worthless souls. He shoos her away callously following which she runs across the street to plead with another passerby she catches sight of. <br /><br />He reaches home, sets his revolver on the table, settles himself down comfortably on his armchair and is ready to carry out the act. He then feels an unexpected pang of regret. He remembers the little girl. He decides he has to settle that matter first. He gropes around his mind to find some justification for his reprehensible deed earlier. Thus contemplating, sleep catches him unawares and he dozes off in his armchair. It is then that he glimpses the truth. An undeniable truth that will change his life and is supposed to change ours. An epiphany within a dream. <br /><br />Here I won't give out any more spoilers. It's important that Dostoyevsky do this job. It should suffice to say that after waking up from this dream, Gloominov gives up on his gloom and takes to the life of a preacher who vows to sing an undying song of love and brotherhood. He preaches forth his dream well knowing that it will be dismissed as a hallucination, as dementia, as naivete. Rightly so, for dismissing it is what I'm going to do anyway.<br /><br />To some extent, the titular ridiculous man is Dostoyevsky himself who was raised in a pious Christian family and fingered the Gospel in his cradle. He is the nihilist turned prophet, the madman, the preacher who is compelled to narrate a tale of truth, love and harmony. For this, he is aware that he is condemned to the ridicule and contempt of the multitude who subscribe to a different version of the truth. The masses who claim that in suffering lies beauty, who love themselves more than others and who utter lies like it is the most natural thing in the world to do. <br /><br />The writing is powerful and the imagery is stunning as Dostoyevsky navigates artfully across the protagonist's disturbed mind. Which is why I give it three stars. However ultimately, this right here is a tale that climbs up a moral pedestal and flatly refuses to get down. At best, it is Christian balm for the soul distressed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14627838253840186903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3395465705" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3395465705" />
    <a title="Greta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111566858-greta"><img alt="Greta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586844759p2/111566858.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3395465705?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greta" href="/user/show/111566858-greta">Greta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781978126763" href="/book/show/36714558-the-dream-of-a-ridiculous-man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111566858-greta?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3395465705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13448692662954798636">“Killing myself was a matter of such indifference to me that I felt like waiting for a moment when it would make some difference.” <br/><br/>This is my favorite quote of Dostoyevsky‘s short story "The dream of a ridiculous Man" and captures its quintessence: the absurdity of human existence in the indifferent universe. Think of the absurdity of pushing a rock up a hill an infinite number of times, like described by Albert Camus in "The myth of sisyphus". <br/><br/>Just like that the narrator of this story thinks of</span>
  <span id="freeText13448692662954798636" style="display:none">“Killing myself was a matter of such indifference to me that I felt like waiting for a moment when it would make some difference.” <br /><br />This is my favorite quote of Dostoyevsky‘s short story "The dream of a ridiculous Man" and captures its quintessence: the absurdity of human existence in the indifferent universe. Think of the absurdity of pushing a rock up a hill an infinite number of times, like described by Albert Camus in "The myth of sisyphus". <br /><br />Just like that the narrator of this story thinks of himself as a failure and his existence as ridiculous and absurd, desperate to the point of suicide. In an attempt to end his life he buys a revolver and plans to shoot himself in the head. First even the decision to pull the trigger seems too indifferent to him to actually execute. But once he finally made the decision to end his life and feels like nothing matters anymore, he refuses to help a child in need, which confuses him, because he realizes that his reaction mattered to someone else.<br /><br />He fell asleep and dreamed that he had killed himself, not by shooting himself in the head, but in the heart. He was buried, without loosing his awareness of his surroundings and then exhumed by a stranger. He traveled to a planet equal to earth, except that it was perfect and untainted, an utopian world. Science and technology were neither known nor necessary. People lived in harmony until his presence began to corrupt the society. He implored the people to crucify him, hoping that his sacrifice would return them to their previous state. Just like it happened to him on earth, the society started to think of him as ridiculous and mad. They threatened him with imprisonment, if he continued ranting about the possibility of an ideal society. He then wakes as a changed man. <br /><br />To me this is a story that confirms the absurdity of human existence and gives thoughts on suicide as a viable response, but at the same time demonstrates that happiness and and meaning can be attained in this world if one understands, that one should do good by his own conscious choosing.<br />As I interpret it, it also comments on the evolution of civilization and christianity. Dostoyevsky stresses that consciousness, knowledge and the ability to choose are what give any sense to life, because humanity had not regressed from paradise but progressed. God only had given the ability to do good, but it was the self given ability to choose to do good that made him progress from being unconscious and instinctive. Therefore living a moral life is not an element of human existence, but knowing to have a choice and that all choices are equal, because they result in the same fate, is the key to all happiness. <br /><br />I absolutely love this!<br /><br />The message I get is that we are merely human beings on this earth who can only conquer the absurdity of our own existence when we understand that our conscious mind transcends everything.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13448692662954798636" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Anaszaidan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4222498-anaszaidan"><img alt="Anaszaidan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325347082p2/4222498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1811521669?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anaszaidan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anaszaidan" href="/user/show/4222498-anaszaidan">Anaszaidan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/14760689">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222498-anaszaidan?shelf=english-books">english-books</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1811521669" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3112667264906912145">سأضع مراجعتي بالإنجليزية،وبعدها باللغة العربية.<br/>I have to talk about my experience in reading the novel before I express my opinion .<br/>I heard it by voice in a terrific performance from a girl through SoundCloud named Amani Issa , and then I saw that I have to read it from a Kindle in English , and discovered new things I wouldn’t have discovered them by reading the serious scientific books , and is that the simple words that I know in English have synonyms that I didn’t hear about them before . Pe</span>
  <span id="freeText3112667264906912145" style="display:none">سأضع مراجعتي بالإنجليزية،وبعدها باللغة العربية.<br />I have to talk about my experience in reading the novel before I express my opinion .<br />I heard it by voice in a terrific performance from a girl through SoundCloud named Amani Issa , and then I saw that I have to read it from a Kindle in English , and discovered new things I wouldn’t have discovered them by reading the serious scientific books , and is that the simple words that I know in English have synonyms that I didn’t hear about them before . Perhaps these are the advantages of reading novels in English .<br />The novel is a combination of desperate background of the Christian Faith . It reminded me of the narrative of some of the stories that were in Bassem magazine , and is to tell you about an exciting adventure to end by the hero to wake up from sleep . This is not defective to Dostoevsky novel , as the difference between the two stories , that the events curve completely change with Dostoevsky when the hero wakes up of slumber , while the glare of story is extinguished as the stories of Bassem Magazine .<br />This desperate miserable man that people make fun of him .. and whenever he wanted to say something , people were laughing …<br />Dostoevsky continues to characterize this sad and harmful scene for the move to the characterization of his feelings and his behavior when he is willing to commit suicide . So he doesn’t mind when a young girl was crying , and he did not make any effort to understand what she said when she was sluttering from the intensity of grief and cold .<br />He decided to commit suicide to move to another life , and then started to mediate his new world , and compared it with the Earth world .<br />Where he met people not mocking him , and really wanted to hear what the hero was saying ... and then proceed with the defamation of those who he met in this world , and he considered himself a victim of the evils of this world .<br />This Characterization is like Christians Characterization to kill Christ Himself on the cross ... Sin is the cause of all that happened and will be .. The hero ends up by asking his colleagues in the other world to crucify him , hoping to get rid of the pain , (it was an intelligent signal from writer , and I consider it an invisible criticism to the idea of sin ... it's about continuing to punish himself because of the sin of human beings and their evils). <br />And then the surprise occurs . The man wakes up from a dream to return to optimism in this life , and begins to rejoice that he is still in this world . And attempts to change his outlook on life and the whole surroundings .<br />The featured in the story , is the writer's ability to meditate on the simple event , making it great at the reader , without exaggeration in the description and boring ... These are the skills of the successful novelist... The turning point in the novel was the look of that miserable after moving to another world , which is the happiness of the surroundings of his existence , and it is free from cynics ... and then discovered a desire to hear others in spite of their lack of understanding what he was saying .<br />The novel is a good Philosophical attempt from Dostoevsky .<br />Personally , I try to make a comparison between what I hear of sermon from the target of people in this life , and what I read from writers who don’t have any Islamic Culture to go out with conclusions that are interesting me .<br />Dostoevsky's philosophy does not depart from the Christian culture , despite imaging crucifixion in a negative way ... He tried in his novel to claim that the solution lies in the distribution of love for everyone ... Just as the Christians (God is love) , (love your enemies) and (Laugh laugh and all the world will be laughing with you) . And all those processors in my opinion are expressing simplicity in visualizing solutions .<br /><br />I'm still in the beginning , both in reading philosophical novels or reading novels in English .. But I will continue to research and make meditation , God willing , until I get to the philosophy of Westerners in their world .. And that novel excited me to search for the views of the critics to understand how they treated with Dostoevsky vision .<br /><br />Reading of the Kindle after adding the amended Arabic translator by one of the members of this beautiful site , and reading another language while driving is so funny .. It also forces you to re-read some lines that you left to understand the meaning .. And this is more beneficial to accommodate the linguistic structures .<br /><br />5 stars are not enough.<br /><br />لا بد أن اتحدث عن تجربتي في قراءة الرواية قبل أن أبدي رأيي فيها. فقد<br />استمعت لها صوتيا بأداء رائع من فتاة، عبر ساوند كلاود، تدعى أماني عيسى.<br /><br />ثم رأيت أن أقرأها من جهاز كيندل بالإنجليزية، فاكتشفت أشياء جديدة لم أكن لاكتشفها جراء قراءتي للكتب العلمية الجادة. وهي أن الكلمات البسيطة التي أعرفها بالإنجليزية لها مرادفات لم أسمع بها قبل ذلك. ولعل هذه من حسنات قراءة الروايات بالإنجليزية.<br /><br />الرواية مزيج  من مشاعر اليائس بخلفية العقيدة المسيحية. ذكرني السرد فيها ببعض القصص التي كانت تطبعها مجلة باسم. وهي أن يحكي لك عن مغامرة شيقة لتنتهي باستيقاظ البطل من النوم، وليس هذا معيب لرواية دستوفيسكي. إذ أن الفارق بين القصتين أن منحنى الأحداث يتغير تماما عند دستوفيسكي عند استيقاظ البطل من غفوته، في حين ينطفئ وهج القصة في أمثال قصص مجلة باسم.<br /><br />هذا اليائس البائس رجل يضحك منه كل الناس..وكلما أراد أن يقول شيئا ضحك منه الناس. يستمر دستوفيسكي في توصيف هذا المشهد الحزين والمؤذي لينتقل إلى توصيف مشاعره وسلوكه وهو راغب في الانتحار. فتجده لا يبالي بفتاة صغيرة تستغيث به..بل لم يبذل جهده كي يفهم ما قالت وهي تتلعثم من شدة الحزن والبرد.<br /><br />يقرر الانتحار لينتقل إلى حياة أخرى..وحينها يبدأ في تأمل عالمه الجديد ويقارنه مع عالم الأرض.إذ يقابل شخصا لا يضحك منه..ويرغب حقا في سماع ما يقوله البطل..ثم يشرع في ذم من قابلهم في دنياه عادا نفسه ضحية شرور ذاك العالم. توصيف يشبه توصيف النصارى لقتل المسيح نفسه على الصليب..فالخطيئة هي سبب كل ما جرى وما سيجري..ينتهي المطاف بالبطل بأن يطلب من رفاقه في العالم الآخر أن يصلبوه أملا بأن يتخلص من آلامه التي حملها،[كانت إشارة ذكية من الكاتب، وأحسبها انتقاد خفي لفكرة الخطيئة..إذ أن المرء يستمر في معاقبة نفسه بسبب خطيئة البشر وشرورهم]. وعندها تحدث المفاجأة..يستيقظ الرجل من منامه ليعود إليه تفاؤله بهذه الحياة، ويبدأ في الابتهاج بأنه ما زال في هذه الدنيا. ويشرع في تغيير نظرته للحياة ولمحيطه كله.<br /><br />المميز في القصة، قدرة دستوفيسكي على التأمل في الحدث البسيط، بما يجعله كبيرا عند القارئ، دون مبالغة في التوصيف الممل..وهذه من مهارات الروائي الناجح.<br />نقطة التحول بالرواية كانت في نظرة ذلك البائس بعد انتقاله لعالم آخر، هي سعادة محيطه بوجوده،وخلوه من الساخرين منه..ثم اكتشافه رغبة الآخرين في سماعه رغم عدم فهمهم لكل ما يقوله.<br /><br />الرواية محاولة فلسفية جيدة من دستوفيسكي. أحاول شخصيا أن أعقد مقارنة بين ما أسمعه من موعظة من غاية البشر من هذه الحياة الدنيا ومما أقرأه من كتاب ليست لديهم ثقافة إسلامية لأخرج باستنتاجات تهمني.<br /><br />فلسفة دستوفيسكي لا تخرج عن إطار الثقافة المسيحية رغم تصويره الصلب بصورة سلبية..فقد حاول في روايته تلك الزعم بأن الحل يكمن في توزيع الحب على الجميع..تماما كما عند النصارى..(الله المحبة)..و(أحبوا أعداءكم) و(اضحك تضحك الدنيا معك). وكل تلك المعالجات برأيي تعبر عن بساطة في تصور الحلول.<br /><br />ما زلت في البدايات..سواء في قراءة الروايات الفلسفية أو في قراءة الروايات باللغة الإنجليزية...ولكن سأواصل البحث والتأمل إن شاء المولى حتى أصل إلى فلسفة الغربيين في دنياهم. وقد حمستني الرواية إلى البحث عن آراء النقاد فيها،لأفهم كيف تعاطوا مع رؤية دستوفيسكي.<br /><br />القراءة من كيندل بعد إضافة المترجم العربي المعدل من قبل أحد اعضاء هذا الموقع جميل. والقراءة بلغة أخرى أثناء قيادة السيارة ممتع أيضا..فهو يجبرك على إعادة قراءة بعض السطور التي تركتها كي تفهم ما وقفت عنده..وهذا أنفع لاستيعاب التراكيب اللغوية.<br /><br />خمس نجوم ليست كافية!</span>

<div id="review_1420940674" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1420940674" />
    <a title="Himanshu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10261330-himanshu"><img alt="Himanshu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594964521p2/10261330.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1420940674?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Himanshu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Himanshu" href="/user/show/10261330-himanshu">Himanshu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Thoughts that never sleep</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10261330-himanshu?shelf=unforgettables">unforgettables</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10261330-himanshu?shelf=hulk-smashed-my-mind">hulk-smashed-my-mind</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10261330-himanshu?shelf=quickies">quickies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10261330-himanshu?shelf=to-re-read">to-re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10261330-himanshu?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10261330-himanshu?shelf=ohh-those-russians">ohh-those-russians</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1420940674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3661384926172419216"><b>
  <u>An Indignant Truth and a Glorified Lie</u>
</b><br/><br/>A man finds himself as if stranded on a place full of people in a mental state that differs only slightly from a coma, because he has the worst disease that a living man can acquire: <i>Indifference</i>. He stands there with a knowledge of how irrevocably misplaced he his and the only logical conclusion that he sees with absolute clarity is to end this lofty astray life of his, to cease the pain of knowledge of this wretched truth that there exists nothing in t</span>
  <span id="freeText3661384926172419216" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>An Indignant Truth and a Glorified Lie</u>
</b><br /><br />A man finds himself as if stranded on a place full of people in a mental state that differs only slightly from a coma, because he has the worst disease that a living man can acquire: <i>Indifference</i>. He stands there with a knowledge of how irrevocably misplaced he his and the only logical conclusion that he sees with absolute clarity is to end this lofty astray life of his, to cease the pain of knowledge of this wretched truth that there exists nothing in this world, there never was, and there never would.<br /><br />He resolves to go home and shoot a bullet through his head. But then he has a sudden recognition of a feeling he hadn't experienced before: <i>Pity</i>. A little girl desperate in need of help clutched his arm on his way home to catch that bullet, but was shooed away with a kick of his indifference. But then when he confronts the revolver, that feeling of pity stares bluntly at him through the bore, questioning its identity because what was its purpose in that indifferent mind of his? Why was it born? Why the shame of his action when he was going to leave this place in some time anyway? Would it matter what he does in these last hours?<br /><br />These questions kindle a flame in the gloomy dreary head of his and acts as a lullaby to that insomniac mind. And for the first time he has an elaborate life altering dream that places him staring at the ultimate naked truth.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Dreams apparently proceed not from reason but from desire, not from the head but from the heart, and yet what clever things my reason has sometimes performed in sleep!</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Tu put it simply, he dreams of a parallel earth where the definition of love disaffiliates itself from suffering. Where people had no desire and no quest for knowledge of sciences, where the emotions of each person was childlike as if the notions of sensuality, and jealousy were alien to them and would never corrupt their love towards everything that was on that emerald planet called Earth. He found this boundless, unbridled love so pure that every other kind seemed like a lie or a sin. He found it as the only <b>Truth</b>.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>...for our science seeks to explain what life is, it aspires to comprehend it, in order to teach others to live; but they know how to live even without science, and I understood that, but I could not understand their knowledge</i>
</blockquote><br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[He then, in his dream, comes to the real earth i.e. our earth of a thousand years before, and he sees that it was he who is at the fault. It is he who has corrupted it all with a lie. <i>They learned to lie and began to love the lie and knew the beauty of the lie</i>. Giving birth to <i>Sensuality</i>.<br /><br /><blockquote><i>that cruel sensuality that overtakes almost everyone on our earth, each and every one, and is the only source of almost all the sins of our mankind.</i></blockquote><br /><br />Thus began an avalanche of all our destructing traits: <i>Jealousy</i>, <i>Cruelty</i>, <i>Shame</i>, <i>Virtue</i>, <i>Honor</i>, and so on. And when he questioned around for the explanation of such a life and its meaning, he came to understand that:<br /><br /><blockquote><i>..we have science, and through it we shall again find the truth, but we shall now accept it consciously, knowledge is higher than feelings, the consciousness of life is higher than life. Science will give us wisdom, wisdom will discover laws, and knowledge of the laws of happiness is higher than happiness</i></blockquote><br /><br />Now he wakes up and like a lightening in the darkest night, he knew that this was the indefatigable <b>Lie</b> that he has to fight. So he forgets, almost detests the idea of his suicide, and starts his journey towards preaching the Truth. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I have marked the above section as spoiler because if you are now even slightly curious, it is only wise to read the rest of it in Doestoevsky's words rather than mine. But, if you know now that you are not going to read it, you can go ahead with that link.<br /><br />I seldom write such reviews where the details of the book are more than my personal reflections, but D didn't give me a choice here. It's as if his version of the truth seems so complete and crystal, that wandering around it wouldn't matter at all. Many contrarian arguments can be made, for ex: it was only a dream, an ideal, a fantasy, a figment of a disillusioned mind. But they will only matter none because, the man has already confessed to being ridiculous and cares the least bit of what the others think of him. He shall only strive to propagate the truth and hope that it reaches the hearts of us pitiful fellow humans. Because who is to say that the life we are living is not but a dream?</span>
  <a data-text-id="3661384926172419216" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1531180611112935730">Dostoyevsky's ideas are hard to be labeled as any sort of philosophy, they're dispersed in a broad spectrum. He has this ability to portray suffering as some sort of delicious sexy looking dish you'd crave for. And all that in twenty-six pages. But suffering is something he talks about a whole lot. He rips the idea of <i>'the paradise'</i>. If you're rich and charming and have a bunch of close friends who'd basically nod at everything you say, have good sex, never hungry, never agitated, completely acc</span>
  <span id="freeText1531180611112935730" style="display:none">Dostoyevsky's ideas are hard to be labeled as any sort of philosophy, they're dispersed in a broad spectrum. He has this ability to portray suffering as some sort of delicious sexy looking dish you'd crave for. And all that in twenty-six pages. But suffering is something he talks about a whole lot. He rips the idea of <i>'the paradise'</i>. If you're rich and charming and have a bunch of close friends who'd basically nod at everything you say, have good sex, never hungry, never agitated, completely accepted and respected, and everyone's optimistic and kind and all that—it doesn't even seem that interesting. What's the whole point? So what's your thing now, just stay happy forever? Perhaps man is attracted to 'suffering' so much that he actually creates them, I guess that's the reason the typical problems of a typical first world citizen and a third world citizen differ so much.</span>

  <span id="freeText4566895384944158982" style="display:none">Faith. This is what emanated from this story the most for me. The faith that love will one day rule every man’s heart. The faith that meaning will be discovered in every action a man performs. The faith that human beings can find and live in truth.<br /><br />A remarkable short story in that it captured my desire to believe in something true, in the goodness of being and the value of experience whether witnessed or imagined. I loved Dostoyevsky’s description of the man’s dream, of being grasped by a force which takes him out of the earth and soaring through the sky to finally land where life is different, where people care for one another unconditionally and can communicate with mother nature in dimensions completely unknown in his previous experience. Beautiful ideas, beautifully conveyed. <br /></span>

It established a template for the genre with a portrait of an isolated man contemptuous of the sordid society around him, paralyzed by doubt, and obsessed with the pain and absurdity of his own existence. Also true to form, the narrative, though it has a plot of sorts, does not redeem its hero in any sense or offer any resolution to his gnawing inner conflict, concluding, literally, as an unfinished text. Thirteen years later, the great Russian writer, his health in decline but his literary reputation and financial prospects much improved, wrote a similar story, “The Dream of a Ridiculous Man.”</i><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.openculture.com/2014/08/dostoevskys-the-dream-of-a-ridiculous-man-animated.html">http://www.openculture.com/2014/08/do...</a></span>

<div id="review_738512744" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/738512744" />
    <a title="Mohnish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15738127-mohnish"><img alt="Mohnish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469268311p2/15738127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/738512744?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mohnish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mohnish" href="/user/show/15738127-mohnish">Mohnish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer738512744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2866806858430160438">This book is about an <i>experience</i> and not a <i>notion</i>, an experience of spiritual enlightenment that I think Dostoevsky had, which he wanted vicariously conveyed through this short story.<br/><br/>The ridiculous man dreams of dying, then gets resurrected or reaches some kind of an afterlife, he lands on this <s>Extra Virgin</s> Greek Island Paradise where everyone is <s>stoned</s> happy, loved & peaceful. People are experiencing eternal bliss but as they consort with our protagonist, they get corrupt, get thrilled by the f</span>
  <span id="freeText2866806858430160438" style="display:none">This book is about an <i>experience</i> and not a <i>notion</i>, an experience of spiritual enlightenment that I think Dostoevsky had, which he wanted vicariously conveyed through this short story.<br /><br />The ridiculous man dreams of dying, then gets resurrected or reaches some kind of an afterlife, he lands on this <s>Extra Virgin</s> Greek Island Paradise where everyone is <s>stoned</s> happy, loved &amp; peaceful. People are experiencing eternal bliss but as they consort with our protagonist, they get corrupt, get thrilled by the first lie they create, this debauchery grows into further evil, proliferating into a world akin to the one we are living in today.<br /><br />Awakening from his sleep the ridiculous man realizes that he had been the cause for the downfall of our species, hence is obliged to preach the ideals of utopia he had encountered in his dream. This bringing direction &amp; purpose to his life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2866806858430160438" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7660081753003607218">This morning in class(!) I misread the text on some kid's shirt as being a book title (Being and Time, in fact) and thought about how strange it would be if people wore shirts advertising their favorite books instead of their favorite bands. Unless people actually do that...I'm pretty sure I've never seen it. That would be really pretentious. <br/>Why would a book shirt seem more pretentious than a band shirt? Is it because reading requires literacy and/or some form of intelligence, and all music req</span>
  <span id="freeText7660081753003607218" style="display:none">This morning in class(!) I misread the text on some kid's shirt as being a book title (Being and Time, in fact) and thought about how strange it would be if people wore shirts advertising their favorite books instead of their favorite bands. Unless people actually do that...I'm pretty sure I've never seen it. That would be really pretentious. <br />Why would a book shirt seem more pretentious than a band shirt? Is it because reading requires literacy and/or some form of intelligence, and all music requires is ears, which are more accessible?<br />I was looking through my books thinking about how funny their titles would look on a t-shirt, and decided that "Dream of a Ridiculous Man" could potentially be the name of some crappy screamo band. <br />Sorry, that was a bad review.</span>

<div id="review_1347897390" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1347897390" />
    <a title="Soycd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16638128-soycd"><img alt="Soycd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527549268p2/16638128.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1347897390?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Soycd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Soycd" href="/user/show/16638128-soycd">Soycd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16638128-soycd?shelf=2016-1">2016-1</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1347897390" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4492415213012625338"> <br/><i>A dream! What is a dream? And is not our life a dream?</i><br/><br/>The dream of a Ridiculous Man is the story of a man who has become disenchanted with the world and is considering suicide. One day he was going home when he was approached by a little girl who asked him for help and he refused to acknowledge her. After that he resumed his walk until he noticed something that caught his eye:<br/><br/><i>The sky was horribly dark, but one could distinctly see tattered clouds, and between them fathomless black patches. Sudd</i></span>
  <span id="freeText4492415213012625338" style="display:none"> <br /><i>A dream! What is a dream? And is not our life a dream?</i><br /><br />The dream of a Ridiculous Man is the story of a man who has become disenchanted with the world and is considering suicide. One day he was going home when he was approached by a little girl who asked him for help and he refused to acknowledge her. After that he resumed his walk until he noticed something that caught his eye:<br /><br /><i>The sky was horribly dark, but one could distinctly see tattered clouds, and between them fathomless black patches. Suddenly I noticed in one of these patches a star, and began watching it intently. That was because that star had given me an idea: I decided to kill myself that night. </i><br /><br />He entered his house and pulled out his gun. He started to think about the little girl until he fell asleep and had a dream that would have a profound impact on him. In that dream he is laying in a coffin after having shot himself in the heart. After that he sees someone taking him out of his coffin. His eyes go black and he is surrounded by a star shinning in the darkness. They travel through space until they reach destination in a planet that looks exactly like Earth.<br /><br /><i>The people of this happy land came to me of themselves, they surrounded me, kissed me. Their faces were radiant with the light of reason and fullness of a serenity that comes of perfect understanding, but those faces were gay; in their words and voices there was a note of childlike joy. Oh, from the first moment, from the first glance at them, I understood it all! It was the earth untarnished by the Fall; on it lived people who had not sinned. </i><br /><br />After that the people who lived there <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[get corrupted, and the protagonist offers to sacrifice himself to redeem them for their sins. He wakes up from the dream as a new man and decides to help the little girl who approached him at the beginning of the story. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />A lot of things were going through my mind when I was reading this. The first one is the religious symbolism in the story. There is the representation of the Garden of Eden, the possibility of suffering to achieve redemption, the idea that the actions of one man can have a huge impact on others and the existence of an afterlife. I think the insistence on the veracity of the dream is an analogy of faith; you have to be willing to believe in things even without proof. The star on the sky may be a representation of fate and hope. The protagonist is a lost soul who needs to reach out to other people to try to find that light amidst the darkness that will allow him to find his own Eden, a place of happiness and understanding.<br /><br />There is something fascinating about Dostoevsky that makes me keep going back to his books: there is so much darkness in his work but the light is always stronger. The message that permeates his whole body of work is his belief in redemption and finding good in people who have lost their way. <br /><br />Reading this author feels like reaching the Eden he describes, it makes you want to become a better person. And after you put the book down you wake up from your own dream and realize that it has been a life changing experience for you too. <br /><br /><i>The chief thing is to love others like yourself, that's the chief thing, and that's everything; nothing else is wanted — you will find out at once how to arrange it all. And yet it's an old truth which has been told and retold a billion times — but it has not formed part of our lives!</i><br /><br />After I finished reading I watched this amazing animation of the story. It was made by Russian director Alexandr Petrov: <br /><br /><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jIr7cs6eEAs" rel="nofollow noopener">Dream of a Ridiculous Man</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4492415213012625338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
